class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Luke Jenkins, Josh Atkins, Dan Black</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>Key Insights</h2>
<ul>
<li>APT29’s pace of operations and emphasis on Ukraine increased in the first half of 2023 as Kyiv launched its counteroffensive, pointing to the SVR’s central role in collecting intelligence concerning the current pivotal phase of the war.</li>
<li>During this period, Mandiant has tracked substantial changes in APT29’s tooling and tradecraft, likely designed to support the increased frequency and scope of operations and hinder forensic analysis.</li>
<li>APT29 has used various infection chains simultaneously across different operations, indicating that distinct initial access operators or subteams are possibly operating in parallel to service different regional targets or espionage objectives.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Threat Detail</h2>
<p>During the lead up to Ukraine's counteroffensive, Mandiant and Google’s Threat Analysis Group (TAG) have tracked an increase in the frequency and scope of APT29 phishing operations. Investigations into the group’s recent activity have identified an intensification of operations centered on foreign embassies in Ukraine. Notably, as part of this activity, we have seen phishing emails targeting a wide range of diplomatic representations in Kyiv including those of Moscow’s partners, representing the first time we have observed this cluster of APT29 activity pursuing governments strategically aligned with Russia. Based on the timing and focus of APT29’s Ukraine-focused operations, we judge they are intended to aid Russia’s Foreign Intelligence Service (SVR) in intelligence collection concerning the current pivotal phase of the war.</p>
<p>APT29’s increased phishing activity in Ukraine has occurred alongside an uptick in the group’s more routine espionage operations against global diplomatic entities. Across these malware delivery operations, APT29 continues to prioritise European Ministries of Foreign Affairs and embassies, but it has also sustained operations that are global in scope and illustrative of Russia’s far-reaching ambitions and interests in other regions. The current secondary focus is concentrated in Asia, with governments in Türkiye (formerly known as Turkey), India, and other regions of vital strategic importance to Moscow such as Africa factoring into its 2023 priorities. We judge that Russia’s war in Ukraine has almost certainly shaped APT29’s espionage priorities, but it has not supplanted them.</p>
<p>We track this diplomatic-focused phishing activity as operationally distinct from APT29’s ongoing initial access operations <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt29-continues-targeting-microsoft" rel="noopener" target="_blank">targeting cloud-based Microsoft products</a>. Although APT29’s cloud-focused exploitation may lead to the compromise of diplomatic entities, variance in the scale, quality and targeting patterns of the two lines of effort indicate that they are highly likely distinct initial access clusters operating with different priorities and levels of capability. However, we continue to see significant overlap in post-compromise methods across both lines of effort, indicating that multiple initial access teams may hand-off to a centralized exploitation team once inside a victim environment.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: APT29’s distinct initial access clusters</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Alongside the increased pace of operations and changes in targeting, we have also seen a major shift in the group’s tooling and tradecraft. APT29 has rebuilt several of its tools and has made repeated iterative modifications to its existing malware delivery chain, likely to ensure its operational longevity despite long-term persistent use. We assess that several of these changes are highly likely specifically designed to sidestep research methods and tools commonly used by the threat intelligence community to track their operations, indicating that operational security priorities continue to factor heavily into APT29’s tooling decisions.</p>
<h2>APT29’s Evolving Approach to Malware Delivery</h2>
<p>Starting in 2021, APT29 adopted a tactic called HTML smuggling in its malware delivery operations, hiding its first-stage JavaScript dropper in malicious HTML attachments we call ROOTSAW (also known as <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/05/28/breaking-down-nobeliums-latest-early-stage-toolset/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>EnvyScout</u></a>). As detailed in previous research by <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/tracking-apt29-phishing-campaigns" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Mandiant</u></a>, <a href="https://www.gov.pl/web/baza-wiedzy/espionage-campaign-linked-to-russian-intelligence-services" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>CERT-Polska</u></a>, <a href="https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/cloaked-ursa-phishing/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Palo Alto Networks</u></a> and others, ROOTSAW has been a constant feature of APT29's operations over the past two years and has been the primary vehicle to decode and deliver the group’s next stage malware. Upon opening the archive file, victims are presented with either a Windows shortcut (LNK) file or a legitimate software binary, that when opened, executes an accompanying DLL, leading to commodity backdoors such as BEACON or BRC4 (Brute Ratel C4) executing on the system.</p>
<p>ROOTSAW’s central and continued role in APT29 operations has spurred changes to the malware delivery chain over time. The most visible change has been the move away from HTML attachments as the initial infection vector, with APT29 shifting to hosting its first-stage payloads on compromised web services such as WordPress sites. Migrating the first-stage payload server side has likely provided APT29 a greater degree of control over its malware delivery chain and allowed the group to be more judicious about the exposure of its later-stage capabilities. For example, to prevent detection of malware in environments not intended for compromise, APT29 has implemented various forms of filtering in its first-stage payloads and has removed staged malware from compromised servers shortly after operational use. Notably, these efforts have also prevented payloads being acquired by public malware repositories and other common security research tools, helping to avoid detection and extend the operational lifespan of its newer malware variants.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 2: APT29’s diverse first-stage delivery methods</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>As shown the following campaigns tracked throughout the first half of 2023 detail, APT29 has made continuous, iterative efforts to introduce additional obfuscation and anti-analysis components into its operations. The group has experimented with various obfuscation techniques such as the use of <a href="https://github.com/javascript-obfuscator/javascript-obfuscator" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>JavaScript Obfuscator</u></a>, delivery and execution guardrails, hosting decryption keys server side, and delivering decoy documents when victim profiling checks fail. In this accelerated period of tooling evolution, the group has also begun to rotate in novel malware delivery tools and techniques instead of its mainstay first-stage payload.</p>
<h3>March 2023: Earthquake-Themed Türkiye Campaign</h3>
<p>In March 2023, Mandiant identified a new APT29 phishing campaign targeting Türkiye. The phishing waves impersonated the Turkish Deputy Minister of Foreign Affairs and included a phishing link accompanied by content related to the February 2023 earthquake that struck southern Türkiye.</p>
<ul>
<li>The first wave, conducted in early March, used a phishing link generated by a URL shortening service “https://tinyurl[.]com/mrxcjsbs” to redirect victims to a ROOTSAW dropper hosted on an actor-controlled compromised website “https://www.willyminiatures[.]com/e-yazi.htm/?v=bc78a8d162c6”.
<ul>
<li>When visited, the URL downloaded the ROOTSAW dropper "e-yazi.htm" (MD5: a3067a0262e651e94329869f43a51722) to drop additional files onto the victim machine, including a malicious ISO, "e-yazi.iso" (MD5: eeded26943a7b2fdef7608fb21bbfd66).</li>
<li>This is the first time Mandiant has seen APT29 introduce an additional layer of obfuscation to its phishing links using a URL shortening service.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>The second wave, victims were directed to an actor-controlled compromised website “https://simplesalsamix[.]com/e-yazi.html” to download the ROOTSAW dropper "e-yazi.html" (MD5: b051e8efb40c2c435d77f3be77c59488).
<ul>
<li>The second ROOTSAW sample dropped similar decoy content and a malicious ZIP file, e-yazi.zip (MD5: 854e5c592e93b69b8ab08dbc8a0b673f), that contained second-stage downloaders and an additional ROOTSAW dropper file.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>In both waves, APT29 incorporated a new version of ROOTSAW with added user-agent based anti-analysis guardrails. This variant checks the user-agent of the device, looking for Windows operating systems that do not contain “.NET” and contain the value “Windows NT”. As a result, if the victim is running a non-Windows based operating system or the request is made through .NET, the server will deliver a decoy PDF file. In the second wave, this PDF file was identical to the version contained in the malicious ZIP payload. This filtering tactic is likely used to identify automated downloaders and non-compatible victim devices, further reducing the odds of exposing malware in non-compromise contexts. Each payload within the new ROOTSAW variant is obfuscated using a unique key, although the deobfuscation routine remains the same.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 3: ROOTSAW user-agent and operating system check to determine which file to deploy</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3>March 2023: European Diplomatic-Focused Phishing Campaigns</h3>
<p>In an additional phishing campaign in March 2023, APT29 targeted various diplomatic missions in Europe. Notably, in the two weeks that lapsed between the Turkey campaign, further changes were identified resulting in two new variants of ROOTSAW that shifted the newly added anti-analysis guardrails server side.</p>
<ul>
<li>In the first wave, emails contained a PDF attachment (MD5: 1485b591e654327c1d032a901940b149) inviting victims to a drink reception following an event on the “Future of International Economic Relations” from the Embassy of Spain. The PDF contains a link to an actor-hosted ROOTSAW variant hosted at “https://parquesanrafael[.]cl/note.html”, ultimately leading to the deployment of MUSKYBEAT (also known publicly as <a href="https://www.gov.pl/attachment/6f51bb1a-3ad2-461c-a16d-408915a56f77" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>QUARTERRIG</u></a>).
<ul>
<li>This version of ROOTSAW sends the victim’s user-agent to the compromised server using an HTTP GET request “https://parquesanrafael[.]cl/note.php?ua=”. The server then performs filtering based on an actor-defined denylist, finally returning a decryption key for the payload if the tests are successfully passed.</li>
<li>If these tests fail, ROOTSAW drops a corrupt file, rather than exposing the embedded decoy file like in previous versions.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>In the second wave, APT29 delivered an additional new variant of ROOTSAW (MD5: 0d5b12c50173a176b0a8ba5a97a831d8), containing both user-agent and IP filtering, but ultimately leading to the same MUSKYBEAT downloader.
<ul>
<li>This version conducts an additional check by obtaining the victim’s IP address through a request to a public API service “https://api.ipify[.]org/?format=json”.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: ROOTSAW payload decryption routine</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3>April 2023: Old Wine in a New Bottle</h3>
<p>In April 2023, APT29 continued to modify its standard malware delivery chain, introducing a new technique for malware delivery. In this operation, APT29 re-used one its frequent diplomatic event-themed lure documents spoofing the Czechia Embassy (more commonly known as the Czech Republic) that invited targets to a wine tasting event on April 13, 2023. The document contained a link to the phishing website “https://sylvio[.]com[.]br/form.php”, which delivered either an ISO or a ZIP archive to the victim.</p>
<ul>
<li>Rather than using ROOTSAW, victims were delivered a malicious ISO or ZIP file directly from the compromised web server if they successfully passed the server-side filtering checks.</li>
<li>The decision to remove the HTML smuggling stage of the infection chain was likely intended to further reduce the number of forensic artifacts left on the host that are prone to detection or later analysis.</li>
</ul>
<h3>May 2023: Ukraine Foreign Embassy-Focused Campaigns</h3>
<p>In May, in the lead up to Ukraine’s counteroffensive, APT29 conducted two distinct phishing waves targeting a wide range of diplomatic representations in Kyiv, including those of Moscow’s partners. Each campaign adopted separate intrusion chains similar to those seen in March and April 2023.</p>
<ul>
<li>In the first wave in early May, an repurposed advert for a BMW sale in Kyiv was circulated, directing victims to an actor-controlled server at “https://resetlocations[.]com/bmw.htm”, which delivered a weaponized ISO file, "bmw.iso" (MD5: e306333093eaf198f4d416d25a40784a).
<ul>
<li>The version of ROOTSAW used in this campaign shares similarities to variants used in March against Türkiye. Depending on user-agent filtering, the ISO or a decoy image of the BMW would be displayed.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>In the second wave mid-May, an invite for a charity concert in Kyiv with a mistyped filename “Invintation.zip” (MD5: 38719acc6254b7ff70dc8a7723bd8e92) was sent to targets, likely also using a copy of a legitimate document.
<ul>
<li>Similar to the April wine-themed campaign, payloads were hosted directly on actor-controlled infrastructure that used user-agent filtering to deliver either a ZIP file with decoy PDF documents (MD5:38719acc6254b7ff70dc8a7723bd8e92), or a ZIP file containing a second-stage payloads (MD5:1aee5bf23edb7732fd0e6b2c61a959ce) to victims.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: Likely repurposed legitimate invite to a charity concert in Ukraine</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3>June 2023: Split ROOTSAW Campaign</h3>
<p>In late June, Mandiant identified an additional APT29 phishing campaign with a new variant of ROOTSAW to target a European government. Phishing emails were sent from a compromised North American government email address and crafted to appear as an invitation to a public holiday celebration from Norwegian embassy personnel. Two different delivery mechanisms were used in this campaign, a PDF (MD5: b4141aa8d234137f0b9549a448158a95) containing a link to an actor-hosted ROOTSAW variant, and emails with an attached Scalable Vector Graphic (SVG) file (MD5: 295527e2e38da97167979ade004de880) rather than the typical HTML payload.</p>
<ul>
<li>Notably, although APT29 used a compromised WordPress server to host the ROOTSAW payload, non-valid targets received a generic HTTP 404 error rather than the traditional WordPress 404.</li>
<li>Mandiant has identified that once APT29 removes the server side functionality, the compromised WordPress site will start displaying the correct WordPress 404 error indicating that the file was not found. We therefore suspect that organisations compromised by APT29 to deliver malware will likely not find logs for this activity within WordPress, although they may exist within other services such as the underlying web server.</li>
<li>The ROOTSAW variant contained in the SVG file is similar to those first identified in 2021, indicating that the threat actor may have only recently adopted SVG files for its HTML smuggling technique. Consistent with other cases where APT29 has introduced new delivery methods for ROOTSAW, the group reverted to a primitive ROOTSAW payload without anti-analysis techniques or other forms of hardening.</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Traditional 404 error from compromised APT29 infrastructure</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 7: 404 error from IP filtered by APT29</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3>July 2023: ICEBEAT Campaign</h3>
<p>In July, APT29 continued to experiment with new ROOTSAW delivery mechanisms and victim filtering capabilities in an operation deploying a new downloader ICEBEAT to target European diplomatic entities. Emails were sent purporting to be an invite from a non-specified German embassy for an Ambassador’s farewell reception. Of note, ICEBEAT’s use of the open source Zulip messaging platform for command and control (C2) follows a pattern of past APT29 downloaders using legitimate services for command and control including Dropbox, Firebase, OneDrive and Trello.</p>
<ul>
<li>For the first time in this campaign, ROOTSAW was contained within a PDF document. When opened, the PDF document writes an HTML file to disk, that when launched, writes a follow-on ZIP file to disk and beacons to an actor controlled domain “https://sgrfh[.]org.pk/wp-content/idx.php?n=ks&amp;q=” to profile victim information.</li>
<li>Victims who met filtering requirements were delivered a Save the date decoy PDF document (MD5: 50f57a4a4bf2c4b504954a36d48c99e7) and delivered the next-stage downloader ICEBEAT that is responsible for downloading follow-on capabilities from the Zulip messaging service.</li>
<li>In instances where the victim did not meet filtering requirements, a separate benign decoy document referencing German Unity Day (MD5: ffce57940b0257a72db4969565cbcebc) was delivered in place of ICEBEAT.</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 8: Decoy lure used by APT29 for filtered victims</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 9: PDF decoy document used during successful malware delivery</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>Malware Choices Possibly Reflect Distinct APT29 Subteams</h2>
<p>Beyond the continued adaptation of APT29’s malware delivery chain, Mandiant has also observed dedicated efforts to update and evolve the group’s later-stage malware into multiple variations, increasing the quantity and quality of tooling used across its campaigns. At least six distinct downloaders have been identified during the first half of 2023:</p>
<ul>
<li>BURNTBATTER is an in-memory loader responsible for decrypting and executing a payload from disk into a running process. BURNTBATTER has been witnessed loading the SPICYBEAT downloader via a position-independent shellcode dropper called DONUT.</li>
<li>DONUT is a publicly available tool that creates position-independent shellcode that loads .NET assemblies, PE files, and other Windows payloads from memory and runs them with parameters.</li>
<li>SPICYBEAT is a downloader written in C++ responsible for downloading a next-stage payload from either DropBox or Microsoft's OneDrive.</li>
<li>MUSKYBEAT is an in-memory dropper that decodes the next-stage payload and strings using RC4 and executes in the current process.</li>
<li>STATICNOISE is a downloader written in C responsible for downloading and executing the final-stage payload in memory.</li>
</ul>
<p>DAVESHELL is shellcode that functions as an in-memory dropper relying on reflective injection. Its embedded payload is mapped into memory and executed. DAVESHELL is based in the <a href="https://github.com/monoxgas/sRDI" rel="noopener" target="_blank">public available repository</a>.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 10: APT29’s Second-Stage Downloaders Used in 2023</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>As noted in the June 2023 campaign, we have also witnessed APT29 operating various infection chains simultaneously within a single campaign, suggesting that distinct initial access operators or subteams may be operating in parallel to service different regional targets or espionage objectives. Although we have been unable to ascertain the specific logic behind decisions about which malware delivery approach to use or when to introduce new later-stage malware variants, we judge with low confidence that they are likely driven by mission-specific parameters such as targets or operational objectives.</p>
<ul>
<li>The first use of new capabilities are typically reserved for targets inside Ukraine or diplomatic entities associated with North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) or European Union (EU) member states, areas of likely heightened strategic importance given Moscow’s need to understand political and military dynamics surrounding its war in Ukraine.</li>
<li>Upon first use of new tactics or tools in higher risk environments, we have observed APT29 incorporate these new tools into its broader operations with minimal changes, pointing to the group’s possible changing risk calculus after first exposure.</li>
<li>Patterns of controlled first-use possibly extend back to the emergence of APT29’s diplomatic-focused phishing cluster in 2021. In May 2021, during Russia’s initial troop-build up around Ukraine, Mandiant identified an APT29 operation using ICEBREAKER, a modified variant of BOOMMIC (also known as <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/05/28/breaking-down-nobeliums-latest-early-stage-toolset/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>VaporRage</u></a>) embedded in software mimicking an installer for a legitimate Ukrainian government application. As detailed by <a href="https://www.sentinelone.com/labs/noblebaron-new-poisoned-installers-could-be-used-in-supply-chain-attacks/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>SentinelOne</u></a>, multiple aspects of the malware delivery chain were likely tailored for a highly-targeted operation against Ukrainian government entities.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Conclusions</h2>
<p>The increased scope and frequency of APT29's diplomatic-focused spear phishing campaigns in the first half of 2023 has compelled the initial access team to make repeated modifications to its long-standing malware delivery chain. Efforts to move capabilities server side, introduce anti-analysis components, and deliver decoy documents in non-compromise contexts have likely helped the group extend the shelf-life of its ROOTSAW-centred concept of operations. Even with this unprecedented pace of change, the group has remained highly operational security conscious, and has taken repeated steps to circumvent the methods that security researchers use to track and respond to its activity.</p>
<p>APT29's increased operational tempo has also exposed patterns of operations that likely reflect different initial access operators or subteams supported by a centralized development team. More generally, these patterns likely reflect a growing mission and pool of resources dedicated to collecting political intelligence and that group will almost certainly continue to pose a high severity threat to governments and diplomatic entities globally.</p>
<h2>Protecting The Community</h2>
<p>As part of our efforts to combat serious threat actors, TAG uses the results of our research to improve the safety and security of Google’s products. Upon discovery, all identified websites and domains are added to <a href="https://safebrowsing.google.com/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Safe Browsing</a> to protect users from further exploitation. TAG also sends all targeted Gmail and Workspace users <a href="https://support.google.com/a/answer/9007870" rel="noopener" target="_blank">government-backed attacker alerts</a> notifying them of the activity and encourages potential targets to enable <a href="https://support.google.com/accounts/answer/11577602" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Enhanced Safe Browsing</u></a> for Chrome and ensure that all devices are updated. Where possible, Mandiant sends victim notifications via the <a href="https://www.mandiant.com/resources/insights/mandiant-victim-notification-program" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><u>Victim Notification Program</u></a>. We are committed to sharing our findings with the security community to raise awareness, and with companies and individuals that might have been targeted by these activities. We hope that improved understanding of tactics and techniques will enhance threat hunting capabilities and lead to stronger user protections across the industry.</p>

<h2>Detection Rules</h2>
<pre><code>rule M_Dropper_BURNTBATTER_1
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        date_created = "2023/04/26"
        description = "Searches for the custom chaskey implementation"
        version = "1"
        weight = "100"
        disclaimer = "This rule is meant for hunting and is not tested to run in a production environment."
    strings:
        $chaskey_imp = {41 81 C8 20 20 20 20 41 81 F8 6B 65 72 6E}
    condition:
        any of them
}</code></pre>
<pre><code>rule M_Dropper_Donut_1
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        date_created = "2023-04-12"
        description = "Detects the structure of the Donut loader"
        version = "1"
        weight = "100"
    condition:
        uint8(0) == 0xE8 and uint32(1) == uint32(5) and uint8(uint32(1)+5) == 0x59
}</code></pre>
<pre><code>rule M_Downloader_STATICNOISE_1
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        date_created = "2023-04-14"
        description = "Detects the deobfuscation algorithm and rc4 from STATICNOISE"
        version = "1"
        weight = "100"
    strings:
        $ = {41 8A C8 48 B8 [8] 80 E1 07 C0 E1 03 48 D3 E8 41 30 04 10 49 FF C0}
        $ = {80 E1 07 C0 E1 03 48 b8 [8] 48 D3 E8 30 04 17 48 FF C7 48 83 FF}
        $ = {40 88 2C 3A 49 8B 02 88 0C 06 45 89 0B 44 89 03 4D 8B 0A}
        $ = {4D 8B 0A 46 0F BE 04 0A 44 03 C1 41 81 E0 FF 00 00 80}
    condition:
        all of them
}</code></pre>
<pre><code>rule M_Dropper_MUSKYBEAT_1 {
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        date_created = "2023-04-06"
        description = "Detects the RC4 encryption algorithm used in MUSKYBEAT"
        version = "1"
        weight = "100"
        disclaimer = "This rule is meant for hunting and is not tested to run in a production environment."
    strings:
        $ = {42 8A 14 04 48 8D ?? ?? ?? ?? ?? 8A C2 41 02 04 08 44 02 D0 41 0F B6 CA}
        $ = {41 B9 04 00 00 00 41 B8 00 30 00 00 48 8B D3 33 C9}
    condition:
        all of them
}</code></pre>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_DaveShell_Dropper_1_2
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        description = "Detects Shellcode RDI projects from  https://github.com/monoxgas/sRDI/blob/master/ShellcodeRDI"
        disclaimer = "This rule is meant for hunting and is not tested to run in a production environment."
    strings:
        $ep = {E8 00 00 00 00 59 49 89 C8 BA [4] 49 81 c0 [4] 41 b9 [4] 56 48 89 e6 48 83 ?? f0 48 83 ec 30 48 89 4c 24 ?? 48 81 c1 [4] c7 44 24 ?? [4] e8}
    condition:
        $ep at 0
}</code></pre>

Operations"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Luke Jenkins, Josh Atkins, Dan Black\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eKey Insights\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAPT29’s pace of operations and emphasis on Ukraine increased in the first half of 2023 as Kyiv launched its counteroffensive, pointing to the SVR’s central role in collecting intelligence concerning the current pivotal phase of the war.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eDuring this period, Mandiant has tracked substantial changes in APT29’s tooling and tradecraft, likely designed to support the increased frequency and scope of operations and hinder forensic analysis.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAPT29 has used various infection chains simultaneously across different operations, indicating that distinct initial access operators or subteams are possibly operating in parallel to service different regional targets or espionage objectives.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eThreat Detail\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDuring the lead up to Ukraine's counteroffensive, Mandiant and Google’s Threat Analysis Group (TAG) have tracked an increase in the frequency and scope of APT29 phishing operations. Investigations into the group’s recent activity have identified an intensification of operations centered on foreign embassies in Ukraine. Notably, as part of this activity, we have seen phishing emails targeting a wide range of diplomatic representations in Kyiv including those of Moscow’s partners, representing the first time we have observed this cluster of APT29 activity pursuing governments strategically aligned with Russia. Based on the timing and focus of APT29’s Ukraine-focused operations, we judge they are intended to aid Russia’s Foreign Intelligence Service (SVR) in intelligence collection concerning the current pivotal phase of the war.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT29’s increased phishing activity in Ukraine has occurred alongside an uptick in the group’s more routine espionage operations against global diplomatic entities. Across these malware delivery operations, APT29 continues to prioritise European Ministries of Foreign Affairs and embassies, but it has also sustained operations that are global in scope and illustrative of Russia’s far-reaching ambitions and interests in other regions. The current secondary focus is concentrated in Asia, with governments in Türkiye (formerly known as Turkey), India, and other regions of vital strategic importance to Moscow such as Africa factoring into its 2023 priorities. We judge that Russia’s war in Ukraine has almost certainly shaped APT29’s espionage priorities, but it has not supplanted them.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe track this diplomatic-focused phishing activity as operationally distinct from APT29’s ongoing initial access operations \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt29-continues-targeting-microsoft\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003etargeting cloud-based Microsoft products\u003c/a\u003e. Although APT29’s cloud-focused exploitation may lead to the compromise of diplomatic entities, variance in the scale, quality and targeting patterns of the two lines of effort indicate that they are highly likely distinct initial access clusters operating with different priorities and levels of capability. However, we continue to see significant overlap in post-compromise methods across both lines of effort, indicating that multiple initial access teams may hand-off to a centralized exploitation team once inside a victim environment.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: APT29’s distinct initial access clusters\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png 971w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png 971w"," 971px,  971px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig1_wkny.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eAlongside the increased pace of operations and changes in targeting, we have also seen a major shift in the group’s tooling and tradecraft. APT29 has rebuilt several of its tools and has made repeated iterative modifications to its existing malware delivery chain, likely to ensure its operational longevity despite long-term persistent use. We assess that several of these changes are highly likely specifically designed to sidestep research methods and tools commonly used by the threat intelligence community to track their operations, indicating that operational security priorities continue to factor heavily into APT29’s tooling decisions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eAPT29’s Evolving Approach to Malware Delivery\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eStarting in 2021, APT29 adopted a tactic called HTML smuggling in its malware delivery operations, hiding its first-stage JavaScript dropper in malicious HTML attachments we call ROOTSAW (also known as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/05/28/breaking-down-nobeliums-latest-early-stage-toolset/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eEnvyScout\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e). As detailed in previous research by \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/tracking-apt29-phishing-campaigns\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eMandiant\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.gov.pl/web/baza-wiedzy/espionage-campaign-linked-to-russian-intelligence-services\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eCERT-Polska\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href\u003d\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/cloaked-ursa-phishing/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003ePalo Alto Networks\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e and others, ROOTSAW has been a constant feature of APT29's operations over the past two years and has been the primary vehicle to decode and deliver the group’s next stage malware. Upon opening the archive file, victims are presented with either a Windows shortcut (LNK) file or a legitimate software binary, that when opened, executes an accompanying DLL, leading to commodity backdoors such as BEACON or BRC4 (Brute Ratel C4) executing on the system.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eROOTSAW’s central and continued role in APT29 operations has spurred changes to the malware delivery chain over time. The most visible change has been the move away from HTML attachments as the initial infection vector, with APT29 shifting to hosting its first-stage payloads on compromised web services such as WordPress sites. Migrating the first-stage payload server side has likely provided APT29 a greater degree of control over its malware delivery chain and allowed the group to be more judicious about the exposure of its later-stage capabilities. For example, to prevent detection of malware in environments not intended for compromise, APT29 has implemented various forms of filtering in its first-stage payloads and has removed staged malware from compromised servers shortly after operational use. Notably, these efforts have also prevented payloads being acquired by public malware repositories and other common security research tools, helping to avoid detection and extend the operational lifespan of its newer malware variants.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: APT29’s diverse first-stage delivery methods\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png 1131w"," 1060px,  1131px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig2_fhum.max-1200x1200.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eAs shown the following campaigns tracked throughout the first half of 2023 detail, APT29 has made continuous, iterative efforts to introduce additional obfuscation and anti-analysis components into its operations. The group has experimented with various obfuscation techniques such as the use of \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/javascript-obfuscator/javascript-obfuscator\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eJavaScript Obfuscator\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, delivery and execution guardrails, hosting decryption keys server side, and delivering decoy documents when victim profiling checks fail. In this accelerated period of tooling evolution, the group has also begun to rotate in novel malware delivery tools and techniques instead of its mainstay first-stage payload.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eMarch 2023: Earthquake-Themed Türkiye Campaign\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn March 2023, Mandiant identified a new APT29 phishing campaign targeting Türkiye. The phishing waves impersonated the Turkish Deputy Minister of Foreign Affairs and included a phishing link accompanied by content related to the February 2023 earthquake that struck southern Türkiye.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe first wave, conducted in early March, used a phishing link generated by a URL shortening service “https://tinyurl[.]com/mrxcjsbs” to redirect victims to a ROOTSAW dropper hosted on an actor-controlled compromised website “https://www.willyminiatures[.]com/e-yazi.htm/?v\u003dbc78a8d162c6”.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWhen visited, the URL downloaded the ROOTSAW dropper \"e-yazi.htm\" (MD5: a3067a0262e651e94329869f43a51722) to drop additional files onto the victim machine, including a malicious ISO, \"e-yazi.iso\" (MD5: eeded26943a7b2fdef7608fb21bbfd66).\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThis is the first time Mandiant has seen APT29 introduce an additional layer of obfuscation to its phishing links using a URL shortening service.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe second wave, victims were directed to an actor-controlled compromised website “https://simplesalsamix[.]com/e-yazi.html” to download the ROOTSAW dropper \"e-yazi.html\" (MD5: b051e8efb40c2c435d77f3be77c59488).\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe second ROOTSAW sample dropped similar decoy content and a malicious ZIP file, e-yazi.zip (MD5: 854e5c592e93b69b8ab08dbc8a0b673f), that contained second-stage downloaders and an additional ROOTSAW dropper file.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn both waves, APT29 incorporated a new version of ROOTSAW with added user-agent based anti-analysis guardrails. This variant checks the user-agent of the device, looking for Windows operating systems that do not contain “.NET” and contain the value “Windows NT”. As a result, if the victim is running a non-Windows based operating system or the request is made through .NET, the server will deliver a decoy PDF file. In the second wave, this PDF file was identical to the version contained in the malicious ZIP payload. This filtering tactic is likely used to identify automated downloaders and non-compatible victim devices, further reducing the odds of exposing malware in non-compromise contexts. Each payload within the new ROOTSAW variant is obfuscated using a unique key, although the deobfuscation routine remains the same.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 3: ROOTSAW user-agent and operating system check to determine which file to deploy\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig3_hnwd.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch3\u003eMarch 2023: European Diplomatic-Focused Phishing Campaigns\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn an additional phishing campaign in March 2023, APT29 targeted various diplomatic missions in Europe. Notably, in the two weeks that lapsed between the Turkey campaign, further changes were identified resulting in two new variants of ROOTSAW that shifted the newly added anti-analysis guardrails server side.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn the first wave, emails contained a PDF attachment (MD5: 1485b591e654327c1d032a901940b149) inviting victims to a drink reception following an event on the “Future of International Economic Relations” from the Embassy of Spain. The PDF contains a link to an actor-hosted ROOTSAW variant hosted at “https://parquesanrafael[.]cl/note.html”, ultimately leading to the deployment of MUSKYBEAT (also known publicly as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.gov.pl/attachment/6f51bb1a-3ad2-461c-a16d-408915a56f77\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eQUARTERRIG\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e).\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThis version of ROOTSAW sends the victim’s user-agent to the compromised server using an HTTP GET request “https://parquesanrafael[.]cl/note.php?ua\u003d”. The server then performs filtering based on an actor-defined denylist, finally returning a decryption key for the payload if the tests are successfully passed.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIf these tests fail, ROOTSAW drops a corrupt file, rather than exposing the embedded decoy file like in previous versions.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn the second wave, APT29 delivered an additional new variant of ROOTSAW (MD5: 0d5b12c50173a176b0a8ba5a97a831d8), containing both user-agent and IP filtering, but ultimately leading to the same MUSKYBEAT downloader.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThis version conducts an additional check by obtaining the victim’s IP address through a request to a public API service “https://api.ipify[.]org/?format\u003djson”.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: ROOTSAW payload decryption routine\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig4.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch3\u003eApril 2023: Old Wine in a New Bottle\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn April 2023, APT29 continued to modify its standard malware delivery chain, introducing a new technique for malware delivery. In this operation, APT29 re-used one its frequent diplomatic event-themed lure documents spoofing the Czechia Embassy (more commonly known as the Czech Republic) that invited targets to a wine tasting event on April 13, 2023. The document contained a link to the phishing website “https://sylvio[.]com[.]br/form.php”, which delivered either an ISO or a ZIP archive to the victim.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eRather than using ROOTSAW, victims were delivered a malicious ISO or ZIP file directly from the compromised web server if they successfully passed the server-side filtering checks.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe decision to remove the HTML smuggling stage of the infection chain was likely intended to further reduce the number of forensic artifacts left on the host that are prone to detection or later analysis.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eMay 2023: Ukraine Foreign Embassy-Focused Campaigns\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn May, in the lead up to Ukraine’s counteroffensive, APT29 conducted two distinct phishing waves targeting a wide range of diplomatic representations in Kyiv, including those of Moscow’s partners. Each campaign adopted separate intrusion chains similar to those seen in March and April 2023.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn the first wave in early May, an repurposed advert for a BMW sale in Kyiv was circulated, directing victims to an actor-controlled server at “https://resetlocations[.]com/bmw.htm”, which delivered a weaponized ISO file, \"bmw.iso\" (MD5: e306333093eaf198f4d416d25a40784a).\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe version of ROOTSAW used in this campaign shares similarities to variants used in March against Türkiye. Depending on user-agent filtering, the ISO or a decoy image of the BMW would be displayed.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn the second wave mid-May, an invite for a charity concert in Kyiv with a mistyped filename “Invintation.zip” (MD5: 38719acc6254b7ff70dc8a7723bd8e92) was sent to targets, likely also using a copy of a legitimate document.\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSimilar to the April wine-themed campaign, payloads were hosted directly on actor-controlled infrastructure that used user-agent filtering to deliver either a ZIP file with decoy PDF documents (MD5:38719acc6254b7ff70dc8a7723bd8e92), or a ZIP file containing a second-stage payloads (MD5:1aee5bf23edb7732fd0e6b2c61a959ce) to victims.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 5: Likely repurposed legitimate invite to a charity concert in Ukraine\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig5_itqp.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch3\u003eJune 2023: Split ROOTSAW Campaign\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn late June, Mandiant identified an additional APT29 phishing campaign with a new variant of ROOTSAW to target a European government. Phishing emails were sent from a compromised North American government email address and crafted to appear as an invitation to a public holiday celebration from Norwegian embassy personnel. Two different delivery mechanisms were used in this campaign, a PDF (MD5: b4141aa8d234137f0b9549a448158a95) containing a link to an actor-hosted ROOTSAW variant, and emails with an attached Scalable Vector Graphic (SVG) file (MD5: 295527e2e38da97167979ade004de880) rather than the typical HTML payload.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eNotably, although APT29 used a compromised WordPress server to host the ROOTSAW payload, non-valid targets received a generic HTTP 404 error rather than the traditional WordPress 404.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMandiant has identified that once APT29 removes the server side functionality, the compromised WordPress site will start displaying the correct WordPress 404 error indicating that the file was not found. We therefore suspect that organisations compromised by APT29 to deliver malware will likely not find logs for this activity within WordPress, although they may exist within other services such as the underlying web server.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe ROOTSAW variant contained in the SVG file is similar to those first identified in 2021, indicating that the threat actor may have only recently adopted SVG files for its HTML smuggling technique. Consistent with other cases where APT29 has introduced new delivery methods for ROOTSAW, the group reverted to a primitive ROOTSAW payload without anti-analysis techniques or other forms of hardening.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: Traditional 404 error from compromised APT29 infrastructure\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png 1072w"," 1060px,  1072px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig6_fgiz.max-1100x1100.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 7: 404 error from IP filtered by APT29\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png 1573w"," 1060px,  1573px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig7_kdno.max-1600x1600.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch3\u003eJuly 2023: ICEBEAT Campaign\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn July, APT29 continued to experiment with new ROOTSAW delivery mechanisms and victim filtering capabilities in an operation deploying a new downloader ICEBEAT to target European diplomatic entities. Emails were sent purporting to be an invite from a non-specified German embassy for an Ambassador’s farewell reception. Of note, ICEBEAT’s use of the open source Zulip messaging platform for command and control (C2) follows a pattern of past APT29 downloaders using legitimate services for command and control including Dropbox, Firebase, OneDrive and Trello.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFor the first time in this campaign, ROOTSAW was contained within a PDF document. When opened, the PDF document writes an HTML file to disk, that when launched, writes a follow-on ZIP file to disk and beacons to an actor controlled domain “https://sgrfh[.]org.pk/wp-content/idx.php?n\u003dks\u0026amp;q\u003d” to profile victim information.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eVictims who met filtering requirements were delivered a Save the date decoy PDF document (MD5: 50f57a4a4bf2c4b504954a36d48c99e7) and delivered the next-stage downloader ICEBEAT that is responsible for downloading follow-on capabilities from the Zulip messaging service.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn instances where the victim did not meet filtering requirements, a separate benign decoy document referencing German Unity Day (MD5: ffce57940b0257a72db4969565cbcebc) was delivered in place of ICEBEAT.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 8: Decoy lure used by APT29 for filtered victims\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png 1182w"," 1060px,  1182px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig8_wrzj.max-1200x1200.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 9: PDF decoy document used during successful malware delivery\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png 1214w"," 1060px,  1214px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig9_zkpy.max-1300x1300.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eMalware Choices Possibly Reflect Distinct APT29 Subteams\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBeyond the continued adaptation of APT29’s malware delivery chain, Mandiant has also observed dedicated efforts to update and evolve the group’s later-stage malware into multiple variations, increasing the quantity and quality of tooling used across its campaigns. At least six distinct downloaders have been identified during the first half of 2023:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eBURNTBATTER is an in-memory loader responsible for decrypting and executing a payload from disk into a running process. BURNTBATTER has been witnessed loading the SPICYBEAT downloader via a position-independent shellcode dropper called DONUT.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eDONUT is a publicly available tool that creates position-independent shellcode that loads .NET assemblies, PE files, and other Windows payloads from memory and runs them with parameters.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSPICYBEAT is a downloader written in C++ responsible for downloading a next-stage payload from either DropBox or Microsoft's OneDrive.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMUSKYBEAT is an in-memory dropper that decodes the next-stage payload and strings using RC4 and executes in the current process.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSTATICNOISE is a downloader written in C responsible for downloading and executing the final-stage payload in memory.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDAVESHELL is shellcode that functions as an in-memory dropper relying on reflective injection. Its embedded payload is mapped into memory and executed. DAVESHELL is based in the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/monoxgas/sRDI\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003epublic available repository\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 10: APT29’s Second-Stage Downloaders Used in 2023\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt29-phishing-fig10_wqlo.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eAs noted in the June 2023 campaign, we have also witnessed APT29 operating various infection chains simultaneously within a single campaign, suggesting that distinct initial access operators or subteams may be operating in parallel to service different regional targets or espionage objectives. Although we have been unable to ascertain the specific logic behind decisions about which malware delivery approach to use or when to introduce new later-stage malware variants, we judge with low confidence that they are likely driven by mission-specific parameters such as targets or operational objectives.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe first use of new capabilities are typically reserved for targets inside Ukraine or diplomatic entities associated with North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) or European Union (EU) member states, areas of likely heightened strategic importance given Moscow’s need to understand political and military dynamics surrounding its war in Ukraine.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUpon first use of new tactics or tools in higher risk environments, we have observed APT29 incorporate these new tools into its broader operations with minimal changes, pointing to the group’s possible changing risk calculus after first exposure.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePatterns of controlled first-use possibly extend back to the emergence of APT29’s diplomatic-focused phishing cluster in 2021. In May 2021, during Russia’s initial troop-build up around Ukraine, Mandiant identified an APT29 operation using ICEBREAKER, a modified variant of BOOMMIC (also known as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/05/28/breaking-down-nobeliums-latest-early-stage-toolset/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eVaporRage\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e) embedded in software mimicking an installer for a legitimate Ukrainian government application. As detailed by \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.sentinelone.com/labs/noblebaron-new-poisoned-installers-could-be-used-in-supply-chain-attacks/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eSentinelOne\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, multiple aspects of the malware delivery chain were likely tailored for a highly-targeted operation against Ukrainian government entities.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eConclusions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe increased scope and frequency of APT29's diplomatic-focused spear phishing campaigns in the first half of 2023 has compelled the initial access team to make repeated modifications to its long-standing malware delivery chain. Efforts to move capabilities server side, introduce anti-analysis components, and deliver decoy documents in non-compromise contexts have likely helped the group extend the shelf-life of its ROOTSAW-centred concept of operations. Even with this unprecedented pace of change, the group has remained highly operational security conscious, and has taken repeated steps to circumvent the methods that security researchers use to track and respond to its activity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT29's increased operational tempo has also exposed patterns of operations that likely reflect different initial access operators or subteams supported by a centralized development team. More generally, these patterns likely reflect a growing mission and pool of resources dedicated to collecting political intelligence and that group will almost certainly continue to pose a high severity threat to governments and diplomatic entities globally.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eProtecting The Community\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs part of our efforts to combat serious threat actors, TAG uses the results of our research to improve the safety and security of Google’s products. Upon discovery, all identified websites and domains are added to \u003ca href\u003d\"https://safebrowsing.google.com/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eSafe Browsing\u003c/a\u003e to protect users from further exploitation. TAG also sends all targeted Gmail and Workspace users \u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/a/answer/9007870\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003egovernment-backed attacker alerts\u003c/a\u003e notifying them of the activity and encourages potential targets to enable \u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/accounts/answer/11577602\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eEnhanced Safe Browsing\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e for Chrome and ensure that all devices are updated. Where possible, Mandiant sends victim notifications via the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.mandiant.com/resources/insights/mandiant-victim-notification-program\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cu\u003eVictim Notification Program\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/a\u003e. We are committed to sharing our findings with the security community to raise awareness, and with companies and individuals that might have been targeted by these activities. We hope that improved understanding of tactics and techniques will enhance threat hunting capabilities and lead to stronger user protections across the industry.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eAppendix\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eATT\u0026amp;CK Matrix\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cdiv
